Abstract Symbolic DistillationOpen sidebarAbstract Symbolic DistillationGPT-4Summarize: [EDIT: there is an ongoing confrontation between physicists, from different areas of expertise, concerning talk of energy conservation in cosmological settings. This post DOES talk in terms of energy conservation. This is in the spirit of how Guth thought about energy when he first came up with the ideas of inflation. The original purpose of writing this was to help me understand what HE meant ... as the subject has developed that way of thinking has gone out of favor ... I'll leave it at that so you can do your own exploration of this topic] 

--------------------------------------------------------------------

One nice way to develop some intuition for the evolution of the expanding universe is to learn a few formulas for the different energy types and then use energy conservation to calculate how the universe will expand with time.

The early universe goes through three distinct periods where the universe is dominated by one of three types of energy:

1. Vacuum energy
2. Radiation energy
3. Matter energy

Common to all three phases though is gravity so we should add:

4. Gravitational energy

So then we can describe the three phases in energy terms:

1. Gravitational energy generating vacuum energy (inflation)
2. Radiation energy losing energy back to gravity (redshift, radiation pressure)
3. Matter energy decoupled from gravitational energy (pressureless expansion)

Using a textbook formula for gravitational energy, I show below how we can use this to calculate the expansion curve for the matter phase. The result is a 2/3 power law:

a(t) â t^(2/3)

This matches our intuition about gravity between masses (at rest) as an attractive force which should slow down the rate of the universe's expansion over time, even though there in no actual energy exchange taking place.1 / 1ChatGPTSummary:There is debate among physicists regarding energy conservation in cosmological contexts. The post explores this concept, referencing how Alan Guth originally conceived energy during the development of inflationary theory. To understand the expansion of the universe, it's essential to consider different energy types dominating distinct phases:Vacuum energyRadiation energyMatter energyGravitational energy (common in all phases)These phases depict:Gravitational energy leading to vacuum energy (inflation).Radiation energy reverting to gravitational energy (manifested as redshift and radiation pressure).Matter energy independent of gravitational energy (undergoing pressureless expansion).Using gravitational energy formulas, the expansion curve for the matter phase can be determined to follow a 2/3 power law: a(t) â t^(2/3). This supports the notion of gravity acting as a decelerative force on the universe's expansion, even without a direct energy exchange.1 / 1It seems that the natural state of the universe is not Minkowski space but de Sitter space.

Certainly it seems that the very early phase of the universe must have been de Sitter like : the inflationary phase.

For reasons largely unknown, that structure broke down: the universe got polluted with quantum particles. Those particles' gravitational attraction distorted the de Sitter structure.

But the underlying de Sitter structure ensured that continued expansion dilutes away those particles, returning the universe to its pure de Sitter structure.

For our own universe that process of dilution took order 10 billion years, so we happen to be living through the beginning of that transition: the so called Cosmic Coincidence.

However, the fundamental de Sitter nature ensures that ongoing expansion will gradually diminish these particles, eventually reverting the universe back to a pure de Sitter state. This may be connected to the gravitational energy's influence, as it impacts all phases of the universe's evolution.In summary, the universe's energy dynamics and its inherent preference for a de Sitter space structure are interconnected, painting a holistic picture of cosmological evolution.1 / 1In mathematical physics, n-dimensional de Sitter space (often abbreviated to dSn) is a maximally symmetric Lorentzian manifold with constant positive scalar curvature. It is the Lorentzian analogue of an n-sphere (with its canonical Riemannian metric).

The main application of de Sitter space is its use in general relativity, where it serves as one of the simplest mathematical models of the universe consistent with the observed accelerating expansion of the universe. More specifically, de Sitter space is the maximally symmetric vacuum solution of Einstein's field equations with a positive cosmological constant {\displaystyle \Lambda }\Lambda  (corresponding to a positive vacuum energy density and negative pressure).

de Sitter space and anti-de Sitter space are named after Willem de Sitter (1872â1934),[1][2] professor of astronomy at Leiden University and director of the Leiden Observatory. Willem de Sitter and Albert Einstein worked closely together in Leiden in the 1920s on the spacetime structure of our universe. de Sitter space was also discovered, independently, and about the same time, by Tullio Levi-Civita.[3]

Definition
de Sitter space can be defined as a submanifold of a generalized Minkowski space of one higher dimension. Take Minkowski space R1,n with the standard metric:

{\displaystyle ds^{2}=-dx_{0}^{2}+\sum _{i=1}^{n}dx_{i}^{2}.}{\displaystyle ds^{2}=-dx_{0}^{2}+\sum _{i=1}^{n}dx_{i}^{2}.}
de Sitter space is the submanifold described by the hyperboloid of one sheet

{\displaystyle -x_{0}^{2}+\sum _{i=1}^{n}x_{i}^{2}=\alpha ^{2},}{\displaystyle -x_{0}^{2}+\sum _{i=1}^{n}x_{i}^{2}=\alpha ^{2},}
where {\displaystyle \alpha }\alpha  is some nonzero constant with its dimension being that of length. The metric on de Sitter space is the metric induced from the ambient Minkowski metric. The induced metric is nondegenerate and has Lorentzian signature. (Note that if one replaces {\displaystyle \alpha ^{2}}\alpha ^{2} with {\displaystyle -\alpha ^{2}}-\alpha ^{2} in the above definition, one obtains a hyperboloid of two sheets. The induced metric in this case is positive-definite, and each sheet is a copy of hyperbolic n-space. For a detailed proof, see Minkowski space Â§ Geometry.)
de Sitter space can also be defined as the quotient O(1, n) / O(1, n â 1) of two indefinite orthogonal groups, which shows that it is a non-Riemannian symmetric space.

Topologically, de Sitter space is R Ã Snâ1 (so that if n â¥ 3 then de Sitter space is simply connected).1 / 1ChatGPTIn mathematical physics, de Sitter space (dS) is an essential concept, particularly in the context of general relativity. with PySR and SymbolicRegression.jl 
Miles Cranmer1,2 
1Princeton University, Princeton, NJ, USA 
2Flatiron Institute, New York, NY, USA 
May 2, 2023 
PySR3is an open-source library for practical symbolic regression, a type of machine learning 
which aims to discover human-interpretable symbolic models. PySR was developed to democra- 
tize and popularize symbolic regression for the sciences, and is built on a high-performance dis- 
tributed backend, a l exible search algorithm, and interfaces with several deep learning packages. 
PySRâs internal search algorithm is a multi-population evolutionary algorithm, which consists 
of a unique evolve-simplify-optimize loop, designed for optimization of unknown scalar constants 
in newly-discovered empirical expressions.PySRâs backend is an extremely optimized Julia li- 
brary SymbolicRegression.jl4, which can be used directly from Julia. It is capable of fusing 
user-def i ned operators into SIMD kernels at runtime, performing automatic dif f erentiation, and 
distributing populations of expressions to thousands of cores across a cluster. In describing this 
software, we also introduce a new benchmark, âEmpiricalBench,â to quantify the applicability of 
symbolic regression algorithms in science. This benchmark measures recovery of historical empirical 
equations from original and synthetic datasets. 
1 Introduction 
Johannes Kepler discovered his famous third law of 
planetary motion, (period)2â (radius)3, from search- 
ing for patterns in thirty years of data produced by 
Tycho Braheâs unaided eye. Kepler did not discover 
this by searching through all conceivable relationships 
with a genetic algorithm on a computer, but by his 
own geometrical intuition. âAnd it was Keplerâs Third 
Law, not an apple, that led Isaac Newton to discover 
the law of gravitationâ [1]. Likewise, Planckâs law was 
not derived from i rst principles, but was a symbolic 
form i t to data [2]. This symbolic relationship would 
inspire the development of Quantum Mechanics. 
Now, this i rst stepâdiscovering empirical rela-

This brings us to an optimization problem known 
as âsymbolic regression,â or SR. In this paper, we de- 
scribe an algorithm for performing SR, and describe 
software dedicated to equation discovery in the sci- 
ences. 
Symbolic Regression.SR describes a supervised 
learning task where the model space is spanned by 
analytic expressions. This is commonly solved in a 
multi-objective optimization framework, jointly min-

tory of science, scientists have often performed SR 
âmanually,â using a combination of their intuition and 
trial-and-error to i nd simple and accurate empirical 
expressions. These empirical expressions then might 
lead to new theoretical insights, such as the afore- 
mentioned discoveries of Kepler and Planck leading 
to classical and quantum mechanics respectively. SR 
algorithms automate this discovery of empirical equa- 
tions, exploiting the power of modern computing to 
test many more expressions than our intuition alone 
could sort through. 
SR saw early developments as a tool for science be- 
ginning in the 1970s and 1980s, with works such as [4], 
and the creation of the tool Bacon in [5] with fur- 
ther development in, e.g., [6, 7]. Via a combination 
of heuristic strategies for brute force searching over 
possible expression trees, Bacon and its follow-ups 
such as Fahrenheit [8], demonstrated the ability to 
discovery a variety of simple physics laws from ideal- 
ized data. The use of genetic algorithms, which allow 
for a more l exible search space and fewer prior as- 
sumptions about these expressions, was popularized 
with the work of [9]. Finally, with the development of 
the user-friendly tools Eureqa in [10, 11, 12], as well 
as HeuristicLab in [13], SR started to become practi- 
cal for use on real-world and noisy scientif i c data and 
empirical equations.1 / 2ChatGPT"Interpretable Machine Learning for Science with PySR and SymbolicRegression.jl" by Miles Cranmer discusses the development and applications of a machine learning tool, PySR, which facilitates symbolic regression (SR). and i nally [35, 36] which apply genetic algorithms to

First, we give an in-depth discussion of the internal 
algorithm in PySR in section 2.1, and then describe

2.1 Algorithm 
PySR is a multi-population evolutionary algorithm 
with multiple evolutions performed asynchronously. 
The main loop of PySR, operating on each popula- 
tion independently, is a classic evolutionary algorithm 
based on tournament selection for individual selection 
(f i rst introduced in the thesis of [55] and generalized 
in [56]), and several mutations and crossovers for gen- 
erating new individuals. Evolutionary algorithms for 
SR was i rst popularized in [9], and has dominated the 
development of l exible SR algorithms since. PySR 
makes several modif i cations to this classic approach, 
some of which are motivated by results in recent work. 
A simple evolutionary algorithm proceeds as fol- 
lows: 1. Assume one has a population of individuals, 
a i tness function, and a set of mutation op- 
erators. 
2. Randomly select an ns-sized subset of indi- 
viduals from the population (e.g., classical

3 
Figure 1: A mutation operation applied to an expres-

Figure 2: A crossover operation between two expres- 
sion trees. 
tournament selection uses ns= 2, but larger 
tournaments are also allowed). 
3. Run the tournament by evaluating the i t- 
ness of every individual in that subset. 
4. Select the i ttest individual as the winner 
with probability p. Otherwise, remove this 
individual from the subset and repeat this 
step again.If there is one remaining, se- 
lect it.Thus, the probability will roughly 
be p,p(1âp),p(1âp)2,... for the i rst, sec- 
ond, third i ttest, and so on. 
5. Create a copy of this selected individual, and 
apply a randomly-selected mutation from a 
set of possible mutations. 
6. Replace a member of the population with 
the mutated individual. Here, one would re- 
place the weakest of the population or sub- 
set. 
This algorithm is computationally ef i cient and al- 
lows for massive parallelization by splitting step 2. 
into groups of individuals which can evolve indepen- 
dently, with asynchronous âmigrationâ between the 
groups.For the i rst of the modif i cations, we re- 
place the eldest member of a population in step 5., 
rather than the individual with the lowest i tness, 
which is known as âage-regularizedâ evolution [57]. 
We record the age from the Unix time at which the 
individual was created during step 5.. Its inclusion in 
PySR was motivated by the impressive Neural Ar- 
chitecture Search results of [57, 58] â though it is 
worth noting similar regularizations used earlier in 
SR, e.g., [59] and [60] â which found this simple 
modif i cation could prevent early convergence without 
hurting performance. This prevents the population 
from specializing too quickly and getting stuck in a 
local minimum of the search space. The implemen- 
tation in [58] uses p = 1 and explores 4 â¤ nsâ¤ 64, 
though here we allow p to be less than 1, among sev- 
eral other dif f erences.

to the change in i tness after mutation. The probabil-

i tness. Since the value of p also controls the diver- 
sity of the population, we can also couple the value 
of p to temperature, setting a larger value of p for 
a higher temperature, and a smaller value of p for 
a lower temperature. Simulated annealing is a sim- 
ple yet powerful strategy for optimizing over discrete spaces, and we i nd in section 3 that simulated an- 
nealing can signif i cantly speed up the search process. 
The second modif i cation we make to tournament 
selection is specif i c to the individuals we wish to 
consider: mathematical expressions. We embed the 
genetic algorithm inside an evolve-simplify-optimize 
loop.âEvolveâ is a repeated application of tourna- 
ment selection-based evolution for a set number of 
mutations. In other words, this is an entire evolu- 
tion of a population. âSimplifyâ here refers to equa- 
5 
tion simplif i cationâequations are simplif i ed to an 
equivalent form during the loop using a set of alge- 
braic equivalencies (since this happens infrequently 
enough, it tends to not harm discovery by constrain- 
ing the search space). The âOptimizeâ part of this al- 
gorithm is a few iterations of a classical optimization 
algorithm (BFGS [63] is used by default, though any 
optimizer in the Optim.jl package can be used [64]) 
to optimize constants in every equation explicitly. 
This part of the algorithm signif i cantly improves 
the discovery of equations containing real constants, 
which is essential for practical application of SR to 
science.It has been shown since at least [65] that 
performing local gradient searches on numerical con- 
stants in expressions can vastly improve the perfor- 
mance of SRâwe closely integrate an optimized and 
stochastic version of this. 
The reason why several mutations (as well as 
crossovers) are performed before proceeding to the 
simplify-optimize stage is because some equations are 
only accessible via a redundant intermediate state. 
For example, xâxâxâx would normally simplify to 
0, but if the simplif i cation is avoided, the next mu- 
tation might change one of the xâs to y, resulting in 
x â x â x â y. Simplifying only occasionally is a way 
of allowing redundant but necessary intermediate ex- 
pressions while at the same time reducing the size of 
the search space. 
Finally, the third modif i cation we make is the use 
of a novel adaptive parsimony metric. But i rst, we 
give a def i nition of complexity. 
Complexity: By default, complexity in PySR 
is equal to the number of nodes in an expression 
tree, regardless of each nodeâs content. 
However, complexity in PySR is completely 
user-conf i gurable. We argue that the best def i- 
nition for a âsimpleâ expression is the one that 
is most interpretable by the user. An expression 
containing a dilogarithm may look commonplace 
to the particle physicistâand in fact be a great 
prior over the space of models for certain parti- 
cle physics dataâbut extremely unusual to the 
l uid dynamicist, and perhaps not coincidentally 
a bad choice of prior for l uids data. 
The traditional mechanism for penalizing complexity 
would be to use a constant parsimony, which would 
involve adding a loss term equal to the complexity of 
an expression times a constant factor: 
â(E) = âpred(E) + (parsimony) Â· C(E),(1) 
for C(E) the complexity of an expression E and âpred 
the predictive loss. Instead, we adaptively tune a per- 
complexity penalty such that the number of expres- 
sions at each complexity is approximately the same. 
This is expressed roughly as: 
â(E) = âpred(E) Â· exp(frecency[C(E)]),(2) 
where the âfrecencyâ of C(E) is some combined mea- 
sure of the frequency and recency of expressions oc- 
curring at complexity C(E) in the population.In 
PySR, we simply count the number of expressions 
with a moving window in time, and divide by a tun- 
able constant.Thus, this is a simple way to pun- 
ish complexities adaptively by how frequent they are 
in the population.This encourages the evolution- 
ary search to explore the problem from simple, less- 
accurate expressions, as well as from complex, more 
accurate expressions. This qualitatively seems to help 
alleviate situations where the search specializes too 
early in the wrong functional form, and can only seem 
to make small iterations to it, and is discouraged from 
starting from scratch.

main evolve-simplify-optimize loop, formalizing parts 
of the cartoon in i g. 3. Algorithm 3 outlines the set

ing. Finally, algorithm 4 describes the tournament

Overview of PySR:PySR is a multi-population evolutionary algorithm.Its primary mechanism is based on a classic evolutionary algorithm utilizing tournament selection for individual selection.2. Classic Evolutionary Algorithm:Starts with a population of individuals.Selects a subset of individuals from the population.Evaluates the fitness of each individual in the subset.Selects the fittest individual as the winner.Makes a copy of the winner and applies a mutation.Replaces a member of the population with the mutated individual.This algorithm can be highly parallelized by evolving different groups of individuals independently.3. Modifications in PySR:Age-regularized Evolution: PySR replaces the oldest member of a population instead of the individual with the lowest fitness.Simulated Annealing: A technique where mutations may be accepted or rejected based on their fitness, aiding the algorithm in not getting stuck in local minima.Evolve-Simplify-Optimize Loop: The 'evolve' step repeatedly applies the evolutionary algorithm, the 'simplify' step condenses the mathematical expressions, and the 'optimize' step refines the constants in the expressions.This loop allows the discovery of various equations, some of which might be accessible only through intermediate redundant states.4. Adaptive Parsimony Metric:PySR introduces an adaptive measure of complexity, where the penalty for complexity is tuned based on the frequency and recency of expressions of similar complexity in the population.This approach helps the algorithm avoid settling too quickly into a specific functional form.5. Pseudocode Explanation:The given text provides pseudocode for the main procedures in PySR, detailing the steps involved in the evolve-simplify-optimize loop, the mutations, and the tournament selection strategy.Overall, PySR offers a robust and flexible algorithmic approach to SR, with the capacity to adapt and optimize based on the unique challenges of each problem space.1 / 1Additional Features.PySR includes a variety of

Let's break down the provided algorithm for PySR step by step:PySR AlgorithmInput:X : Dataset for which symbolic expressions are to be found.Parameters:np : Number of populations, default is 40.L : Number of expressions in each population, default is 1000.Î±H : Replacement fraction using expressions from H, default is 0.05.Î±M : Replacement fraction using expressions from union of Mi, default is 0.05.Algorithm Steps:Initialization:For each population i in the range of total populations (np):Create an initial set Pi containing L random symbolic expressions, each of complexity 3. For instance, the expression (3.2 + x1) has a size of 3.Initialize an empty set Mi. This will store the best expressions encountered in the population Pi.Initialize an empty set H that will store the best expressions overall across all populations.Evolution Loop:The outer loop runs for niter iterations (though niter is not specified in the provided algorithm). Within this loop:Each population Pi undergoes the evolve-simplify-optimize process in parallel:Evolve: Modify Pi based on evolutionary strategies to get better expressions.For each expression E in Pi:Simplify: Reduce the complexity of E without changing its meaning.Optimize: Adjust constants in E to better fit the data.Update the set Mi with the most accurate expression in Pi at each complexity level. In reality, Mi is updated continuously during the evolve process.Update the global set H with the most accurate expression from the union of all Mi and H at each complexity.Migration Process: This helps in sharing good expressions between populations.For each expression E in a population Pi:With probability Î±H, replace E in Pi with a random expression from the global set H.With probability Î±M, replace E in Pi with a random expression from another population Mj (j not equal to i).Output:After all iterations are complete, return the set H, which contains the best symbolic expressions for the dataset X across all complexities.In essence: The algorithm aims to find the best symbolic expressions to represent the data X. It does this by evolving multiple populations of expressions in parallel. The best expressions from each population are shared with other populations and globally to ensure convergence to an optimal solution.1 / 1Algorithm 2: Evolution.

Choosing the appropriate operators and setting a complexity level are essential parts of ensuring that the discovered expressions are both accurate and interpretable.Choosing Operators:Built-in Operators: These are standard mathematical operators like addition, subtraction, multiplication, division, etc. PySR would inherently understand these and can use them in formulating expressions.Custom Operators: As mentioned in the provided text, PySR's flexibility shines here. Different scientific domains might have specific functions or operations that are commonly used. By allowing users to define their custom unary or binary functions, PySR can create expressions that make sense within the context of specific scientific domains. For instance, a physicist might want to include a Bessel function as an operator because it frequently appears in physics-related equations.The key takeaway here is that PySR doesn't discriminate between built-in and user-defined operators when formulating expressions. Both are treated equally, with the only distinction being in simplification strategies.Setting Complexity Level:In symbolic regression, an expression's complexity can be seen as the number of operators, operands, and functions that constitute it. A simple expression like x+2x + 2x+2 has lower complexity than a more convoluted expression like sinâ¡(x2+3xâ4)\sin(x^2 + 3x - 4)sin(x2+3xâ4).Why is complexity important? There's a balance to strike. Overly simple expressions might not fit the data well, leading to high error. However, very complex expressions might overfit the data, capturing noise rather than the underlying relationship. Such overfitted models perform poorly on unseen data. Therefore, setting a desired level of complexity helps in controlling the trade-off between underfitting and overfitting.In PySR, constraints on expression size (which relates to complexity) can be enforced. If an expression exceeds the maximum allowed size or complexity, it can be pruned or rejected during the evolutionary search process.In summary, choosing the right operators and setting an appropriate complexity level are vital decisions in symbolic regression. They determine the types of expressions that will be considered and the balance between simplicity and accuracy. Properly setting these parameters helps in finding models that are both interpretable and accurate.1 / 1Algorithm 4: Tournament selection.

The method works by selecting a subset of the population and choosing the "fittest" individual from that subset.Here's a step-by-step explanation of Algorithm 4:Function: tournament(P,X)Input: A population of expressions P and a dataset X.Output: A single expression, the winner of the tournament.Steps in the tournament function:Step 2: Select a random subset Q from the population P with size ns (which is 12, as per the given parameters).Step 3-9: Continually find the fittest expression from the subset Q and potentially remove it until only one expression remains or a stopping condition (based on ptournament) is met.Step 4: Call the get_fittest function to retrieve the fittest expression E from the current subset Q.Step 5: There's a probability ptournament (given as 0.9) to immediately break out of the loop and select E as the winner.Step 8: If the loop doesn't break, then the current fittest expression E is removed from the subset Q.Step 10: Return the final expression E, which is the winner of the tournament.Function: get_fittest(P)Input: A set of expressions P.Output: The fittest expression from P.Steps in the get_fittest function:Step 13-23: Iterate over each expression E in P to find the fittest one.Step 15: Calculate the complexity of expression E.Step 16: Determine the accuracy â of expression E.Step 17-18: Apply adaptive parsimony to the accuracy. Parsimony is a method used in genetic programming to prevent overfitting and encourages simpler solutions. The frecency parameter adjusts the accuracy based on the complexity C of the expression, acting as a penalty for more complex expressions.Step 19-22: Compare the modified accuracy â of expression E with the best accuracy observed so far (âbest). If E is better (i.e., has a lower adjusted accuracy), it becomes the new best candidate Eâ.Step 24: Return the fittest expression Eâ.In summary, the tournament function is a method of selecting individual expressions based on their fitness. The fitness in this context is determined by an expression's accuracy and complexity. The tournament continues until only one expression remains, or a predefined probability condition is met, at which point that expression is returned as the winner.1 / 12.2 Software implementation 
The search algorithm itself underlying PySR, as de- 
scribed in pseudocode in algorithms 1 to 4, is written 
in pure-Julia under the library name SymbolicRe- 
gression.jl8. Julia boasts a combination of a high- 
level interface9with very high performance compara- 
ble to languages such as C++ and Rust10. However, 
the key advantage of using Julia for this work is the 
fact that it is a just-in-time compiled (JIT) language. 
This allows PySR to make use of optimizations not 
possible with a statically compiled library: for in- 
stance, compiling user-def i ned operators and losses 
into the core functions. The most signif i cant advan- 
tage of using JIT compilation for PySR, in terms of 
performance, is that operators can be fused together 
into single compiled operators. For example, if a user 
declares + and- as valid operators, then PySR will 
compile a SIMD-enabled kernel for +, -, as well as 
their combination: (a- b) + c, and so on. This 
happens automatically for every single combination 
of operators up to a depth of two operators, even for 
user-def i ned operators. Simply by fusing operators in 
this way, the expression evaluation code, which re- 
mains the bottleneck in PySR, experiences a signif- 
icant speedup. Because Julia is JIT compiled, these 
operators need not be pre-def i ned in the library: they 
will be just as performant as if they were. 
PySR exposes a simple Python frontend API 
tothispure-Juliabackendsearchalgorithm.

Here's a breakdown of the given text:Software Implementation Overview:Library and Language:The search algorithm of PySR is implemented in a library named SymbolicRegression.jl using the Julia programming language.Julia combines the ease of a high-level interface with performance comparable to languages like C++ and Rust.Advantages of Julia:Julia is a just-in-time compiled (JIT) language. This JIT nature allows for on-the-fly optimizations that can't be done with statically compiled libraries.A major benefit for PySR is that operators (both built-in and user-defined) can be combined or "fused" into single compiled operators. For instance, if '+' and '-' are defined as valid operators, PySR can automatically compile a combined operation like (a - b) + c.This fusion of operations, especially up to a depth of two operators, provides a significant speed boost. The reason is that evaluating symbolic expressions is often the performance bottleneck in symbolic regression.Julia's JIT compilation ensures that these fused operators perform optimally, even if they aren't predefined in the library.Python Frontend:While the core algorithm is written in Julia, PySR provides a user-friendly Python API.The style of this frontend API is inspired by the popular scikit-learn machine learning library.Usage Example:The given example showcases how to use PySR in Python.Initialization: Use PySRRegressor to create a symbolic regression model. You can specify options like model selection strategy and which unary and binary operators to consider during the search.Loading Data: Data is loaded with shapes (n_rows, n_features) for input X and (n_rows) or (n_rows, n_targets) for output y.Training: The fit method starts the symbolic regression search on the provided data.Results: Discovered symbolic expressions can be viewed by printing the model object.Prediction: Use the predict method to make predictions using discovered expressions. You can specify which expression to use based on its position in the Pareto front (a concept in multi-objective optimization where solutions are ranked based on trade-offs between objectives, like simplicity and accuracy in this context).In summary, PySR is a bridge between Julia and Python, leveraging Julia's performance capabilities while providing the ease and familiarity of Python's interface. The JIT nature of Julia enables optimizations, particularly the fusion of operations, which drastically enhances performance, making symbolic regression more efficient and effective.1 / 1It is also very important to note that these results are

4 Conclusion 
In this paper, we have presented PySR, an open- 
source library for practical symbolic regression. We 
have developed PySR with the aim of democratizing 
and popularizing symbolic regression in the sciences. 
By leveraging a powerful multi-population evolu- 
tionary algorithm, a unique evolve-simplify-optimize 
loop, a high-performance distributed backend, and in- 
tegration with deep learning packages, PySR is ca- 
pable of accelerating the discovery of interpretable 
symbolic models from data. 
Furthermore, through the introduction of a new 
benchmark, EmpiricalBench, we have provided a 
means to quantitatively evaluate the performance of 
symbolic regression algorithms in scientif i c applica- 
tions. 
Our results indicate that, despite advances in 
deep learning, classic algorithms build on evolution 
and other untrained symbolic techniques such as 
PySR, Operon, DSR, and QLattice, still outperform 
pure deep-learning-based approaches, EQL and SR- 
Transformer, in discovering historical empirical equa- 
17 
tions from original and synthetic datasets. This high- 
lights the challenges faced by deep learning methods 
when applied to real-world data with its biases and 
heteroscedasticity, and the nonlinearity of the space 
of expressions.

techniquesâinclusive of generative models like [30],

symbolic distillation [23]âall hold potential for im- 
proving symbolic regression, and encourage further 
exploration of such hybrid methods.

Incorporating reinforcement learning (RL) can be a critical step in refining the products based on taste tests. Here's how this can be integrated:Environment Setup:In the context of RL, the environment is the product itself (e.g., a meat or cheese replacement). Every time the algorithm proposes a new formulation, it's like taking an action in the environment.Feedback as Reward:After a taste test, feedback can be quantified into a reward signal. Positive feedback (e.g., "tastes like real meat") can be given a positive reward, while negative feedback (e.g., "too grainy" or "lacks flavor") can result in a negative reward or penalty.Model Update:Based on the reward, the RL agent updates its model, trying to maximize the reward for its next action (i.e., the next product formulation). The goal of the agent is to find the formulation that maximizes cumulative rewards (positive feedback from taste tests).Exploration vs. Exploitation:A critical aspect of RL is balancing exploration (trying entirely new formulations) with exploitation (refining promising ones). This ensures that while the algorithm can hone in on a good recipe, it also has the freedom to discover potentially better alternatives.Iterative Improvement:This process of proposing formulations, gathering feedback, and updating the model continues iteratively. Over time, the RL agent becomes better at proposing product formulations that are likely to be well-received during taste tests.Incorporate Other Data:While taste is paramount, other data like texture, appearance, and nutritional content can also be incorporated into the reward function, ensuring that the final product is not just tasty but also visually appealing and nutritionally balanced.Policy Improvement:After several iterations, the policy (strategy) that the RL agent uses to propose formulations becomes more refined. Once the policy is deemed satisfactory, it can be "frozen" and used to design products without further RL, until there's a need to develop new variants.Integrating RL into the product development process can make the process more adaptive and responsive to user feedback. Moreover, by continuously refining the algorithm's model, the time to develop a satisfactory product could be shortened significantly, saving resources and accelerating market entry.1 / 1It sounds complicated could you give a potential pipeline?1 / 1ChatGPTCertainly! Here's a potential pipeline for this process:Understand the Model's Objective:Before distilling insights, it's essential to have a clear understanding of what the model was designed to achieve. This will set the context for any derived insights.Feature Importance:Use techniques like SHAP (SHapley Additive exPlanations), permutation importance, or feature importance from tree-based models to identify which features (or variables) have the most influence on the model's predictions.Model Simplification:One common method is to train a simpler model, such as a linear regression or decision tree, to approximate the predictions of the complex model. This simpler model can then be inspected to gain insights.Visualization:Visual tools can help in understanding complex models. Heatmaps, partial dependence plots, and other graphical methods can show how variables interact and influence model outputs.Rule Extraction:Techniques like decision trees or rule-based algorithms can be applied to extract decision rules from complex models.Surrogate Models:Train a simpler, interpretable model (the surrogate) to approximate the complex model's outputs. By analyzing the surrogate, you can gain insights into the original model.Sensitivity Analysis:Investigate how slight changes in input features affect the model's output. This can provide insights into how different features influence the model's decisions.Error Analysis:By examining instances where the model errs, you can gain insights into its limitations and potential biases.Case Studies:Dive deep into specific instances or predictions made by the model. By understanding why the model made a particular prediction in a given case, you can often generalize and gain broader insights.Feedback Loop with Domain Experts:Engaging with experts in the field can provide valuable insights. They can help validate findings, provide context, or even identify areas where the model might be making incorrect assumptions.Regularization Techniques:Techniques like L1 (Lasso) regularization can help in making models sparser, reducing the number of influential features and making them more interpretable.Symbolic Regression (like PySR):This can be used to derive symbolic mathematical equations that capture the relationships in the data, providing a more human-readable insight.By combining these methods and continuously iterating based on feedback and validation, it becomes possible to distill complex models into meaningful scientific insights. However, it's also crucial to remember that no single method will fit all scenarios, and the approach should be tailored based on the specific model and domain.1 / 1Could you give an analogy for each step as a kind of narrative journey?1 / 1ChatGPTCertainly!
